<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Hippocrates</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Ancient Greek physician (c. 460 – c. 370 BC)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Hippocrates_(disambiguation)" title="Hippocrates (disambiguation)">Hippocrates (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox biography vcard"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:125%;"><div class="fn" style="display:inline">Hippocrates of Kos</div></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hippocrates.jpg"><img alt="Hippocrates.jpg" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="483" decoding="async" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Hippocrates.jpg/220px-Hippocrates.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Hippocrates.jpg/330px-Hippocrates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Hippocrates.jpg/440px-Hippocrates.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a><div class="infobox-caption">A conventionalized image in a Roman "portrait" bust (19th-century engraving)</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Born</th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="circa">c.</span> 460 BC<br/><div class="birthplace" style="display:inline"><a href="/wiki/Kos" title="Kos">Kos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Died</th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="circa">c.</span> 370 BC<br/>(aged approximately 90)<br/><div class="deathplace" style="display:inline"><a href="/wiki/Larissa" title="Larissa">Larissa</a>, Ancient Greece</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Occupation</th><td class="infobox-data role"><a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">Physician</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Era</th><td class="infobox-data category"><a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Greece</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Hippocrates of Kos</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ἱπποκράτης ὁ Κῷος</span></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">translit.</a></small> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn"><i>Hippokrátēs ho Kôios</i></i></span>; <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 460</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 370 BC</span>), also known as <b>Hippocrates II</b>, was a Greek <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">classical period</a> who is considered one of the most outstanding figures in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine" title="History of medicine">history of medicine</a>. He is traditionally referred to as the "Father of Medicine" in recognition of his lasting contributions to the field, such as the use of <a href="/wiki/Prognosis" title="Prognosis">prognosis</a> and clinical observation, the systematic categorization of diseases, or the formulation of <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">humoural theory</a>. The Hippocratic school of medicine revolutionized <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_medicine" title="Ancient Greek medicine">ancient Greek medicine</a>, establishing it as a discipline distinct from other fields with which it had traditionally been associated (<a href="/wiki/Theurgy" title="Theurgy">theurgy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>), thus establishing medicine as a profession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison9293_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-garrison9293-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nuland5_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-nuland5-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the achievements of the writers of the <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Corpus" title="Hippocratic Corpus">Hippocratic Corpus</a>, the practitioners of Hippocratic medicine, and the actions of Hippocrates himself were often conflated; thus very little is known about what Hippocrates actually thought, wrote, and did. Hippocrates is commonly portrayed as the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/paragon" title="wikt:paragon">paragon</a> of the ancient physician and credited with coining the <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">Hippocratic Oath</a>, which is still relevant and in use today. He is also credited with greatly advancing the systematic study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_medicine" title="Clinical medicine">clinical medicine</a>, summing up the medical knowledge of previous schools, and prescribing practices for physicians through the Hippocratic Corpus and other works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison9293_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-garrison9293-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hippocrate_refusant_les_pr%C3%A9sents_d%27Artaxerx%C3%A8s_(original).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1113" data-file-width="1539" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hippocrate_refusant_les_pr%C3%A9sents_d%27Artaxerx%C3%A8s_%28original%29.JPG/330px-Hippocrate_refusant_les_pr%C3%A9sents_d%27Artaxerx%C3%A8s_%28original%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hippocrate_refusant_les_pr%C3%A9sents_d%27Artaxerx%C3%A8s_%28original%29.JPG/495px-Hippocrate_refusant_les_pr%C3%A9sents_d%27Artaxerx%C3%A8s_%28original%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hippocrate_refusant_les_pr%C3%A9sents_d%27Artaxerx%C3%A8s_%28original%29.JPG/660px-Hippocrate_refusant_les_pr%C3%A9sents_d%27Artaxerx%C3%A8s_%28original%29.JPG 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hippocrate_refusant_les_pr%C3%A9sents_d%27Artaxerx%C3%A8s_(original).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the story of Hippocrates refusing the presents of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid" title="Achaemenid">Achaemenid</a> Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II_of_Persia" title="Artaxerxes II of Persia">Artaxerxes</a>, who was asking for his services. Painted by <a href="/wiki/Anne-Louis_Girodet_de_Roussy-Trioson" title="Anne-Louis Girodet de Roussy-Trioson">Girodet</a>, 1792.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Historians agree that Hippocrates was born around the year 460 BC on the Greek island of <a href="/wiki/Kos" title="Kos">Kos</a>; other biographical information, however, is likely to be untrue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Soranus_of_Ephesus" title="Soranus of Ephesus">Soranus of Ephesus</a>, a 2nd-century Greek <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BCE_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-BCE-6">[6]</a></sup> was Hippocrates' first biographer and is the source of most personal information about him. Later biographies are in the <i><a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a></i> of the 10th century AD, and in the works of <a href="/wiki/John_Tzetzes" title="John Tzetzes">John Tzetzes</a>, which date from the 12th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison9293_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-garrison9293-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nuland7_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-nuland7-7">[7]</a></sup> Hippocrates is mentioned in passing in the writings of two contemporaries: Plato, in <i><a href="/wiki/Protagoras_(dialogue)" title="Protagoras (dialogue)">Protagoras</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedrus_(dialogue)" title="Phaedrus (dialogue)">Phaedrus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics</a></i>, which date from the 4th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soranus wrote that Hippocrates' father was <a href="/wiki/Heraclides_(physician)" title="Heraclides (physician)">Heraclides</a>, a physician, and his mother was Praxitela, daughter of Tizane. The two sons of Hippocrates, <a href="/wiki/Thessalus_(physician)" title="Thessalus (physician)">Thessalus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Draco_(physician)" title="Draco (physician)">Draco</a>, and his son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Polybus_(physician)" title="Polybus (physician)">Polybus</a>, were his students. According to <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, a later physician, Polybus, was Hippocrates' true successor, while Thessalus and Draco each had a son named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hippocrates_(physician)" title="Hippocrates (physician)">Hippocrates</a> (Hippocrates III and IV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams19_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-adams19-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-margotta66_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-margotta66-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soranus said that Hippocrates learned medicine from his father and grandfather (<a href="/wiki/Hippocrates_(physicians)" title="Hippocrates (physicians)">Hippocrates I</a>), and studied other subjects with <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gorgias" title="Gorgias">Gorgias</a>. Hippocrates was probably trained at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asclepieion" title="Asclepieion">asklepieion</a> of Kos, and took lessons from the <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thracian</a> physician <a href="/wiki/Herodicus" title="Herodicus">Herodicus of Selymbria</a>. <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> mentions Hippocrates in two of his dialogues: in <i><a href="/wiki/Protagoras_(dialogue)" title="Protagoras (dialogue)">Protagoras</a></i>, Plato describes Hippocrates as "Hippocrates of Kos, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asclepiad_(Greek)" title="Asclepiad (Greek)">Asclepiad</a>";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marti86_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-marti86-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> while in <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedrus_(dialogue)" title="Phaedrus (dialogue)">Phaedrus</a></i>, Plato suggests that "Hippocrates the Asclepiad" thought that a complete knowledge of the nature of the body was necessary for medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Hippocrates taught and practiced medicine throughout his life, traveling at least as far as <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">Sea of Marmara</a>. Several different accounts of his death exist. He died, probably in <a href="/wiki/Larissa" title="Larissa">Larissa</a>, at the age of 83, 85 or 90, though some say he lived to be well over 100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-margotta66_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-margotta66-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hippocratic_theory">Hippocratic theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Hippocratic theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023981488">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .rquote{width:auto!important;float:none!important}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: right; width: 33%;"><p>It is thus with regard to the disease called Sacred: it appears to me to be nowise more divine nor more sacred than other diseases, but has a natural cause from the originates like other affections. Men regard its nature and cause as divine from ignorance and wonder....</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Hippocrates, <i>On the Sacred Disease</i></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Hippocrates is credited with being the first person to believe that diseases were caused naturally, not because of superstition and gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philosophy_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-philosophy-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones11_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-jones11-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nuland8_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-nuland8-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison9394_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-garrison9394-18">[18]</a></sup> Hippocrates was credited by the disciples of <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> of allying philosophy and medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philosophy_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-philosophy-15">[15]</a></sup> He separated the discipline of medicine from religion, believing and arguing that disease was not a punishment inflicted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_gods" title="Greek gods">gods</a> but rather the product of environmental factors, diet, and living habits. Indeed there is not a single mention of a mystical illness in the entirety of the Hippocratic Corpus. However, Hippocrates did work with many convictions that were based on what is now known to be incorrect <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">Humorism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones11_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-jones11-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nuland8_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-nuland8-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison9394_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-garrison9394-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancient Greek schools of medicine were split (into the Knidian and Koan) on how to deal with disease. The <a href="/wiki/Knidos" title="Knidos">Knidian</a> school of medicine focused on diagnosis.  Medicine at the time of Hippocrates knew almost nothing of human anatomy and physiology because of the Greek <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboo</a> forbidding the dissection of humans. The Knidian school consequently failed to distinguish when one disease caused many possible series of symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams15_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-adams15-19">[19]</a></sup> The Hippocratic school or <a href="/wiki/Kos" title="Kos">Koan</a> school achieved greater success by applying general <a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">diagnoses</a> and passive treatments. Its focus was on patient care and <a href="/wiki/Prognosis" title="Prognosis">prognosis</a>, not diagnosis. It could effectively treat diseases and allowed for a great development in clinical practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-margotta67_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-margotta67-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hippocratic medicine and its philosophy are far removed from that of modern medicine. Now, the physician focuses on specific diagnosis and specialized treatment, both of which were espoused by the Knidian school. This shift in medical thought since Hippocrates' day has caused serious criticism over their denunciations; for example, the <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> doctor M. S. Houdart called the Hippocratic treatment a "meditation upon death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones1213_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-jones1213-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Analogies have been drawn between Thucydides' historical method and the Hippocratic method, in particular the notion of "human nature" as a way of explaining foreseeable repetitions for future usefulness, for other times or for other cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crisis">Crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kos_Asklepeion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="876" data-file-width="1453" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Kos_Asklepeion.jpg/220px-Kos_Asklepeion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Kos_Asklepeion.jpg/330px-Kos_Asklepeion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Kos_Asklepeion.jpg/440px-Kos_Asklepeion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kos_Asklepeion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asklepieion" title="Asklepieion">Asklepieion</a> on <a href="/wiki/Kos" title="Kos">Kos</a></div></div></div>
<p>An important concept in Hippocratic medicine was that of a <i>crisis</i>, a point in the progression of disease at which either the illness would begin to triumph and the patient would succumb to death, or the opposite would occur and natural processes would make the patient recover. After a crisis, a relapse might follow, and then another deciding crisis. According to this doctrine, crises tend to occur on <i>critical days</i>, which were supposed to be a fixed time after the contraction of a disease. If a crisis occurred on a day far from a <i>critical day</i>, a relapse might be expected. Galen believed that this idea originated with Hippocrates, though it is possible that it predated him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones464859_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-jones464859-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HippocraticBench.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="347" data-file-width="687" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/HippocraticBench.png/300px-HippocraticBench.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/HippocraticBench.png/450px-HippocraticBench.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/HippocraticBench.png/600px-HippocraticBench.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HippocraticBench.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of a <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_bench" title="Hippocratic bench">Hippocratic bench</a>, date unknown</div></div></div>
<p>Hippocratic medicine was humble and passive. The therapeutic approach was based on "the healing power of nature" ("<i><a href="/wiki/Vis_medicatrix_naturae" title="Vis medicatrix naturae">vis medicatrix naturae</a></i>" in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>). According to this doctrine, the body contains within itself the power to re-balance the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_humours" title="Four humours">four humours</a> and heal itself (<i>physis</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison99_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-garrison99-25">[25]</a></sup> Hippocratic therapy focused on simply easing this natural process. To this end, Hippocrates believed "rest and immobilization [were] of capital importance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-margotta73_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-margotta73-26">[26]</a></sup> In general, the Hippocratic medicine was very kind to the patient; treatment was gentle, and emphasized keeping the patient clean and sterile. For example, only clean water or wine were ever used on wounds, though "dry" treatment was preferable. Soothing <a href="/wiki/Liniment" title="Liniment">balms</a> were sometimes employed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison98_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-garrison98-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hippocrates was reluctant to administer drugs and engage in specialized treatment that might prove to be wrongly chosen; generalized therapy followed a generalized diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison98_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-garrison98-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sing35_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-sing35-28">[28]</a></sup> Generalized treatments he prescribed include fasting and the consumption of a mix of honey and vinegar. Hippocrates once said that "to eat when you are sick, is to feed your sickness". However, potent drugs were used on certain occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-29">[29]</a></sup> This passive approach was very successful in treating relatively simple ailments such as broken bones which required <a href="/wiki/Traction_(orthopedics)" title="Traction (orthopedics)">traction</a> to stretch the skeletal system and relieve pressure on the injured area. The <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_bench" title="Hippocratic bench">Hippocratic bench</a> and other devices were used to this end.
</p><p>One of the strengths of Hippocratic medicine was its emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Prognosis" title="Prognosis">prognosis</a>. At Hippocrates' time, medicinal therapy was quite immature, and often the best thing that physicians could do was to evaluate an illness and predict its likely progression based upon data collected in detailed case histories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison9394_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-garrison9394-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison97_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-garrison97-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Professionalism">Professionalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Professionalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancientgreek_surgical.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="757" data-file-width="907" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ancientgreek_surgical.jpg/220px-Ancientgreek_surgical.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ancientgreek_surgical.jpg/330px-Ancientgreek_surgical.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ancientgreek_surgical.jpg/440px-Ancientgreek_surgical.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ancientgreek_surgical.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A number of ancient Greek surgical tools. On the left is a <a href="/wiki/Trephine" title="Trephine">trephine</a>; on the right, a set of <a href="/wiki/Scalpel" title="Scalpel">scalpels</a>. Hippocratic medicine made good use of these tools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams17_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-adams17-31">[31]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Hippocratic medicine was notable for its strict professionalism, discipline, and rigorous practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-garrison-32">[32]</a></sup> The Hippocratic work <i>On the Physician</i> recommends that physicians always be well-kempt, honest, calm, understanding, and serious. The Hippocratic physician paid careful attention to all aspects of his practice: he followed detailed specifications for, "lighting, personnel, instruments, positioning of the patient, and techniques of bandaging and splinting" in the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operating_room" title="Operating room">operating room</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-margotta64_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-margotta64-33">[33]</a></sup> He even kept his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fingernail" title="Fingernail">fingernails</a> to a precise length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rutkow24_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-rutkow24-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Hippocratic School gave importance to the clinical doctrines of observation and documentation. These doctrines dictate that physicians record their findings and their medicinal methods in a very clear and objective manner, so that these records may be passed down and employed by other physicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-margotta66_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-margotta66-11">[11]</a></sup> Hippocrates made careful, regular note of many symptoms including complexion, pulse, fever, pains, movement, and excretions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison97_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-garrison97-30">[30]</a></sup> He is said to have measured a patient's pulse when taking a case history to discover whether the patient was lying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marti88_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-marti88-35">[35]</a></sup> Hippocrates extended clinical observations into family history and environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-margotta68_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-margotta68-36">[36]</a></sup> "To him medicine owes the art of clinical inspection and observation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison9394_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-garrison9394-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_contributions_to_medicine">Direct contributions to medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Direct contributions to medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ClubbingFingers1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1572" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/ClubbingFingers1.jpg/220px-ClubbingFingers1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/ClubbingFingers1.jpg/330px-ClubbingFingers1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/ClubbingFingers1.jpg/440px-ClubbingFingers1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ClubbingFingers1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clubbing of fingers in a patient with <a href="/wiki/Eisenmenger%27s_syndrome" title="Eisenmenger's syndrome">Eisenmenger's syndrome</a>; first described by Hippocrates, clubbing is also known as "Hippocratic fingers".</div></div></div>
<p>Hippocrates and his followers were first to describe many diseases and medical conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> He is given credit for the first description of <a href="/wiki/Nail_clubbing" title="Nail clubbing">clubbing</a> of the fingers, an important diagnostic sign in chronic lung disease, <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanotic_heart_defect" title="Cyanotic heart defect">cyanotic heart disease</a>. For this reason, clubbed fingers are sometimes referred to as "Hippocratic fingers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schwartz_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-schwartz-38">[38]</a></sup> Hippocrates was also the first physician to describe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hippocratic_face" title="Hippocratic face">Hippocratic face</a> in <i>Prognosis</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a> famously alludes to this description when writing of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falstaff" title="Falstaff">Falstaff</a>'s death in Act II, Scene iii. of <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_V_(play)" title="Henry V (play)">Henry V</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sing40_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-sing40-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-margotta70_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-margotta70-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hippocrates began to categorize illnesses as <a href="/wiki/Acute_(medicine)" title="Acute (medicine)">acute</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronic_(medicine)" title="Chronic (medicine)">chronic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">endemic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemic</a>, and use terms such as, "exacerbation, <a href="/wiki/Relapse" title="Relapse">relapse</a>, resolution, crisis, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paroxysm" title="Paroxysm">paroxysm</a>, peak, and <a href="/wiki/Convalescence" title="Convalescence">convalescence</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison97_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-garrison97-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mart90_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-mart90-41">[41]</a></sup> Another of Hippocrates' major contributions may be found in his descriptions of the symptomatology, physical findings, surgical treatment and prognosis of <a href="/wiki/Empyema" title="Empyema">thoracic empyema</a>, i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suppuration" title="Suppuration">suppuration</a> of the lining of the chest cavity. His teachings remain relevant to present-day students of <a href="/wiki/Pulmonology" title="Pulmonology">pulmonary medicine</a> and surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-major_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-major-42">[42]</a></sup> Hippocrates was the first documented <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiothoracic_Surgery" title="Cardiothoracic Surgery">chest surgeon</a> and his findings and techniques, while crude, such as the use of lead pipes to drain chest wall abscess, are still valid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-major_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-major-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Hippocratic school of medicine described well the ailments of the human <a href="/wiki/Rectum" title="Rectum">rectum</a> and the treatment thereof, despite the school's poor theory of medicine. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemorrhoids" title="Hemorrhoids">Hemorrhoids</a>, for instance, though believed to be caused by an excess of bile and phlegm, were treated by Hippocratic physicians in relatively advanced ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johann11_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-johann11-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jani_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-jani-44">[44]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cautery" title="Cautery">Cautery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">excision</a> are described in the Hippocratic Corpus, in addition to the preferred methods: <a href="/wiki/Ligature_(medicine)" title="Ligature (medicine)">ligating</a> the hemorrhoids and drying them with a hot iron. Other treatments such as applying various salves are suggested as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johann12_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-johann12-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-book-46">[46]</a></sup> Today, "treatment [for hemorrhoids] still includes burning, strangling, and excising."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johann11_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-johann11-43">[43]</a></sup> Also, some of the fundamental concepts of <a href="/wiki/Proctoscopy" title="Proctoscopy">proctoscopy</a> outlined in the Corpus are still in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johann11_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-johann11-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jani_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-jani-44">[44]</a></sup> For example, the uses of the rectal <a href="/wiki/Speculum_(medical)" title="Speculum (medical)">speculum</a>, a common medical device, are discussed in the Hippocratic Corpus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jani_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-jani-44">[44]</a></sup> This constitutes the earliest recorded reference to <a href="/wiki/Endoscopy" title="Endoscopy">endoscopy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Hippocrates often used lifestyle modifications such as <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exercise" title="Exercise">exercise</a> to treat diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, what is today called <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_medicine" title="Lifestyle medicine">lifestyle medicine</a>. 
</p><p>Two popular but likely misquoted attributions to Hippocrates are "Let food be your medicine, and medicine be your food" and "Walking is man's best medicine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Both appear to be misquotations, and their exact origins remain unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, researchers claimed that, while conducting restorations on the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Catherine%27s_Monastery" title="Saint Catherine's Monastery">Saint Catherine's Monastery</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Sinai" title="South Sinai">South Sinai</a>, they found a manuscript which contains a medical recipe of Hippocrates. The manuscript also contains three recipes with pictures of herbs that were created by an anonymous scribe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hippocratic_Corpus">Hippocratic Corpus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Hippocratic Corpus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Corpus" title="Hippocratic Corpus">Hippocratic Corpus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HippocraticOath.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="983" data-file-width="696" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HippocraticOath.jpg/220px-HippocraticOath.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HippocraticOath.jpg/330px-HippocraticOath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HippocraticOath.jpg/440px-HippocraticOath.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HippocraticOath.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 12th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_empire" title="Byzantine empire">Byzantine</a> manuscript of the Oath in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Cross" title="Cross">cross</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Hippocratic Corpus (Latin: <i>Corpus Hippocraticum</i>) is a collection of around seventy early medical works collected in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandrian_period" title="Alexandrian period">Alexandrian Greece</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iniesta2011_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iniesta2011-53">[53]</a></sup> It is written in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionic_dialect" title="Ionic dialect">Ionic Greek</a>. The question of whether Hippocrates himself was the author of any of the treatises in the corpus has not been conclusively answered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singer27_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-singer27-54">[54]</a></sup> but current debate revolves around only a few of the treatises seen as potentially authored by him. Because of the variety of subjects, writing styles and apparent date of construction, the Hippocratic Corpus could not have been written by one person (Ermerins numbers the authors at nineteen).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-29">[29]</a></sup> The corpus came to be known by his name because of his fame, possibly all medical works were classified under 'Hippocrates' by a librarian in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marti86_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-marti86-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-margotta64_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-margotta64-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The volumes were probably produced by his students and followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanson_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-hanson-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Hippocratic Corpus contains textbooks, lectures, research, notes and philosophical essays on various subjects in medicine, in no particular order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singer27_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-singer27-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rutkow23_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-rutkow23-57">[57]</a></sup> These works were written for different audiences, both specialists and laymen, and were sometimes written from opposing viewpoints; significant contradictions can be found between works in the Corpus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sing28_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-sing28-58">[58]</a></sup> Notable among the treatises of the Corpus are <i><a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">The Hippocratic Oath</a></i>; <i>The Book of Prognostics</i>; <i>On Regimen in Acute Diseases</i>; <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Aphorisms" title="s:Aphorisms">Aphorisms</a></i>; <i>On Airs, Waters and Places</i>; <i>Instruments of Reduction</i>; <i>On The Sacred Disease</i>; etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hippocratic_Oath">Hippocratic Oath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hippocratic Oath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">Hippocratic Oath</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">Hippocratic Oath</a>, a seminal document on the <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a> of medical practice, was attributed to Hippocrates in antiquity although new information shows it may have been written after his death. This is probably the most famous document of the Hippocratic Corpus. Recently the authenticity of the document's author has come under scrutiny. While the Oath is rarely used in its original form today, it serves as a foundation for other, similar <a href="/wiki/Oath" title="Oath">oaths</a> and laws that define good medical practice and morals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wma.net_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-wma.net-59">[59]</a></sup> Such derivatives are regularly taken today by medical graduates about to enter medical practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marti86_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-marti86-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones217_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-jones217-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galenoghippokrates.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Galenoghippokrates.jpg/220px-Galenoghippokrates.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Galenoghippokrates.jpg/330px-Galenoghippokrates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Galenoghippokrates.jpg/440px-Galenoghippokrates.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galenoghippokrates.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mural painting showing <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> and Hippocrates. 12th century; <a href="/wiki/Anagni" title="Anagni">Anagni</a>, Italy</div></div></div>
<p>Although Hippocrates neither founded the school of medicine named after him, nor wrote most of the treatises attributed to him, he is traditionally regarded as the "Father of Medicine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> His contributions revolutionized the practice of medicine; but after his death the advancement stalled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison100_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-garrison100-63">[63]</a></sup> So revered was Hippocrates that his teachings were largely taken as too great to be improved upon and no significant advancements of his methods were made for a long time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marti86_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-marti86-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-margotta73_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-margotta73-26">[26]</a></sup> The centuries after Hippocrates' death were marked as much by retrograde movement as by further advancement. For instance, "after the Hippocratic period, the practice of taking clinical case-histories died out," according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fielding_H._Garrison" title="Fielding H. Garrison">Fielding Garrison</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison95_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-garrison95-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>After Hippocrates, another significant physician was <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> who lived from AD 129 to AD 200. Galen perpetuated the tradition of Hippocratic medicine, making some advancements, but also some regressions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones35_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-jones35-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, the Islamic world adopted Hippocratic methods and developed new medical technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Renaissance" title="European Renaissance">European Renaissance</a>, Hippocratic methods were revived in western Europe and even further expanded in the 19th century. Notable among those who employed Hippocrates' rigorous clinical techniques were <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sydenham" title="Thomas Sydenham">Thomas Sydenham</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Heberden" title="William Heberden">William Heberden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Martin_Charcot" title="Jean-Martin Charcot">Jean-Martin Charcot</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Osler" title="William Osler">William Osler</a>. <a href="/wiki/Henri_Huchard" title="Henri Huchard">Henri Huchard</a>, a French physician, said that these revivals make up "the whole history of internal medicine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison934_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-garrison934-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Image">Image</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Image">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Engraving;_bust_of_Hippocrates;_by_Paul_Wellcome_L0019959.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1658" data-file-width="1218" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Engraving%3B_bust_of_Hippocrates%3B_by_Paul_Wellcome_L0019959.jpg/220px-Engraving%3B_bust_of_Hippocrates%3B_by_Paul_Wellcome_L0019959.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Engraving%3B_bust_of_Hippocrates%3B_by_Paul_Wellcome_L0019959.jpg/330px-Engraving%3B_bust_of_Hippocrates%3B_by_Paul_Wellcome_L0019959.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Engraving%3B_bust_of_Hippocrates%3B_by_Paul_Wellcome_L0019959.jpg/440px-Engraving%3B_bust_of_Hippocrates%3B_by_Paul_Wellcome_L0019959.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Engraving;_bust_of_Hippocrates;_by_Paul_Wellcome_L0019959.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Engraving: bust of Hippocrates by <a href="/wiki/Paulus_Pontius" title="Paulus Pontius">Paulus Pontius</a> after <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Peter Paul Rubens</a>, 1638</div></div></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s testimony, Hippocrates was known as "The Great Hippocrates".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones38_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-jones38-69">[69]</a></sup> Concerning his disposition, Hippocrates was first portrayed as a "kind, dignified, old country doctor" and later as "stern and forbidding".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marti86_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-marti86-12">[12]</a></sup> He is certainly considered wise, of very great intellect and especially as very practical. <a href="/wiki/Francis_Adams_(translator)" title="Francis Adams (translator)">Francis Adams</a> describes him as "strictly the physician of experience and common sense."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams15_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-adams15-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>His image as the wise, old doctor is reinforced by busts of him, which wear large beards on a wrinkled face. Many physicians of the time wore their hair in the style of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jove</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asklepius" title="Asklepius">Asklepius</a>. Accordingly, the busts of Hippocrates that have been found could be only altered versions of portraits of these deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison100_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-garrison100-63">[63]</a></sup> Hippocrates and the beliefs that he embodied are considered medical ideals. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fielding_Garrison" title="Fielding Garrison">Fielding Garrison</a>, an authority on medical history, stated, "He is, above all, the exemplar of that flexible, critical, well-poised attitude of mind, ever on the lookout for sources of error, which is the very essence of the scientific spirit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garrison934_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-garrison934-68">[68]</a></sup> "His figure... stands for all time as that of the ideal physician," according to <i>A Short History of Medicine</i>, inspiring the medical profession since his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sing29_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-sing29-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legends">Legends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Legends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Sir_John_Mandeville" title="The Travels of Sir John Mandeville">The Travels of Sir John Mandeville</a></i> reports (incorrectly) that Hippocrates was the ruler of the islands of "Kos and Lango" [sic], and recounts a legend about Hippocrates' daughter. She was transformed into a hundred-foot long <a href="/wiki/Dragon" title="Dragon">dragon</a> by the goddess <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Diana</a>, and is the "lady of the manor" of an old castle. She emerges three times a year, and will be turned back into a woman if a knight kisses her, making the knight into her consort and ruler of the islands. Various knights try, but flee when they see the hideous dragon; they die soon thereafter. This is a version of the legend of <a href="/wiki/Melusine" title="Melusine">Melusine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Namesakes">Namesakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Namesakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hippocrates_sculpture_in_front_of_Mayne_Medical_School,_Brisbane,_2021.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3906" data-file-width="2841" decoding="async" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Hippocrates_sculpture_in_front_of_Mayne_Medical_School%2C_Brisbane%2C_2021.jpg/220px-Hippocrates_sculpture_in_front_of_Mayne_Medical_School%2C_Brisbane%2C_2021.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Hippocrates_sculpture_in_front_of_Mayne_Medical_School%2C_Brisbane%2C_2021.jpg/330px-Hippocrates_sculpture_in_front_of_Mayne_Medical_School%2C_Brisbane%2C_2021.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Hippocrates_sculpture_in_front_of_Mayne_Medical_School%2C_Brisbane%2C_2021.jpg/440px-Hippocrates_sculpture_in_front_of_Mayne_Medical_School%2C_Brisbane%2C_2021.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hippocrates_sculpture_in_front_of_Mayne_Medical_School,_Brisbane,_2021.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Hippocrates in front of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Queensland_Mayne_Medical_School" title="University of Queensland Mayne Medical School">Mayne Medical School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a></div></div></div>
<p>Some clinical symptoms and signs have been named after Hippocrates as he is believed to be the first person to describe those. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hippocratic_face" title="Hippocratic face">Hippocratic face</a> is the change produced in the countenance by death, or long sickness, excessive evacuations, excessive hunger, and the like. <a href="/wiki/Nail_clubbing" title="Nail clubbing">Clubbing</a>, a deformity of the fingers and fingernails, is also known as Hippocratic fingers. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocratic_succussion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hippocratic succussion (page does not exist)">Hippocratic succussion</a> is the internal splashing noise of <a href="/wiki/Hydropneumothorax" title="Hydropneumothorax">hydropneumothorax</a> or <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pyopneumothorax&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pyopneumothorax (page does not exist)">pyopneumothorax</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_bench" title="Hippocratic bench">Hippocratic bench</a> (a device which uses tension to aid in setting bones) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocratic_cap-shaped_bandage&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hippocratic cap-shaped bandage (page does not exist)">Hippocratic cap-shaped bandage</a> are two devices named after Hippocrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fishchenko_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fishchenko-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Corpus" title="Hippocratic Corpus">Hippocratic Corpus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">Hippocratic Oath</a> are also his namesakes. <a href="/wiki/Risus_sardonicus" title="Risus sardonicus">Risus sardonicus</a>, a sustained spasming of the face muscles may also be termed the Hippocratic Smile. The most severe form of hair loss and baldness is called the Hippocratic form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the modern age, a lunar crater has been named <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates_(lunar_crater)" title="Hippocrates (lunar crater)">Hippocrates</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Museum" title="Hippocratic Museum">Hippocratic Museum</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Museum" title="Museum">museum</a> on the Greek island of Kos is dedicated to him. <a href="/wiki/The_Hippocrates_Project" title="The Hippocrates Project">The Hippocrates Project</a> is a program of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a> Medical Center to enhance education through use of technology. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Project Hippocrates (page does not exist)">Project Hippocrates</a> (an acronym of "<b>HI</b>gh <b>P</b>erf<b>O</b>rmance <b>C</b>omputing for <b>R</b>obot-<b>A</b>ssis<b>TE</b>d <b>S</b>urgery") is an effort of the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_School_of_Computer_Science" title="Carnegie Mellon School of Computer Science">Carnegie Mellon School of Computer Science</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shadyside_Medical_Center&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shadyside Medical Center (page does not exist)">Shadyside Medical Center</a>, "to develop advanced planning, simulation, and execution technologies for the next generation of computer-assisted surgical robots."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-project_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-project-74">[74]</a></sup>  Both the <a class="external text" href="http://www.hippocraticregistry.com" rel="nofollow">Canadian Hippocratic Registry</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=American_Hippocratic_Registry&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="American Hippocratic Registry (page does not exist)">American Hippocratic Registry</a> are organizations of physicians who uphold the principles of the original Hippocratic Oath as inviolable through changing social times.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genealogy">Genealogy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Genealogy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Hippocrates' legendary genealogy traces his paternal heritage directly to Asklepius and his maternal ancestry to <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-29">[29]</a></sup> According to Tzetzes's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiliades" title="Chiliades">Chiliades</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Ahnentafel" title="Ahnentafel">ahnentafel</a> of Hippocrates II is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-adams-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HSAsclepiusKos_retouched.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="495" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/HSAsclepiusKos_retouched.jpg/220px-HSAsclepiusKos_retouched.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/HSAsclepiusKos_retouched.jpg/330px-HSAsclepiusKos_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/HSAsclepiusKos_retouched.jpg/440px-HSAsclepiusKos_retouched.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HSAsclepiusKos_retouched.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> of Hippocrates on the floor of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asclepieion" title="Asclepieion">Asclepieion</a> of Kos, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asklepius" title="Asklepius">Asklepius</a> in the middle, 2nd–3rd century</div></div></div>

</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hippocrates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
